<ul><li><img alt="Category" data-file-height="31" data-file-width="36" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/48/Folder_Hexagonal_Icon.svg/16px-Folder_Hexagonal_Icon.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/48/Folder_Hexagonal_Icon.svg/24px-Folder_Hexagonal_Icon.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/48/Folder_Hexagonal_Icon.svg/32px-Folder_Hexagonal_Icon.svg.png 2x" title="Category" width="16"/> <a href="/wiki/Category:Syndicalism" title="Category:Syndicalism">Syndicalism</a></li>
<li><img alt="Category" data-file-height="31" data-file-width="36" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/48/Folder_Hexagonal_Icon.svg/16px-Folder_Hexagonal_Icon.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/48/Folder_Hexagonal_Icon.svg/24px-Folder_Hexagonal_Icon.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/48/Folder_Hexagonal_Icon.svg/32px-Folder_Hexagonal_Icon.svg.png 2x" title="Category" width="16"/> <a href="/wiki/Category:Labor_economics" title="Category:Labor economics">Labour economics</a></li></ul></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Syndicalism_sidebar" title="Template:Syndicalism sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Syndicalism_sidebar" title="Template talk:Syndicalism sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Syndicalism_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Green anarchism</b> (or <b>eco-anarchism</b>) is a <a href="/wiki/Anarchist_schools_of_thought" title="Anarchist schools of thought">school of thought</a> within <a href="/wiki/Anarchism" title="Anarchism">anarchism</a> which puts a particular emphasis on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Environmental_issues" title="Environmental issues">environmental issues</a>. A green anarchist theory is normally one that extends anarchist ideology beyond a critique of human interactions, and includes a critique of the interactions between humans and non-humans as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> This often culminates in an anarchist revolutionary praxis that is not merely dedicated to human liberation, but also to some form of nonhuman liberation,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> and that aims to bring about an environmentally sustainable anarchist society.
</p><p>Important early influences were <a href="/wiki/Henry_David_Thoreau" title="Henry David Thoreau">Henry David Thoreau</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leo_Tolstoy" title="Leo Tolstoy">Leo Tolstoy</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-naturismolibertario_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-naturismolibertario-3">[3]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/%C3%89lis%C3%A9e_Reclus" title="Élisée Reclus">Élisée Reclus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reclus1_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reclus1-4">[4]</a></sup> In the late 19th century there emerged <a href="/wiki/Anarcho-naturism" title="Anarcho-naturism">anarcho-naturism</a> as the fusion of anarchism and <a href="/wiki/Naturism" title="Naturism">naturist</a> philosophies within <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Individualist_anarchist" title="Individualist anarchist">individualist anarchist</a> circles in France, Spain, Cuba,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-raforum.info_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-raforum.info-5">[5]</a></sup> and Portugal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-naturismolibertario_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-naturismolibertario-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spanishind_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-spanishind-6">[6]</a></sup> Important contemporary currents (some of which may be mutually exclusive) include <a href="/wiki/Anarcho-primitivism" title="Anarcho-primitivism">anarcho-primitivism</a>, which offers a critique of technology and argues that anarchism is best suited to uncivilised ways of life, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Green_syndicalism" title="Green syndicalism">Green syndicalism</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Green_politics" title="Green politics">Green</a> anarcho-socialist political stance made up of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anarcho_syndicalism" title="Anarcho syndicalism">anarcho-syndicalist</a> views, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Veganarchism" title="Veganarchism">veganarchism</a>: which argues that human liberation and animal liberation are inseparable;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Social_ecology_(theory)" title="Social ecology (theory)">social ecology</a>, which argues that the hierarchical domination of nature by human stems from the hierarchical domination of human by human.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_ecoanarchism">Early ecoanarchism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Green_anarchism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early ecoanarchism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Henry_David_Thoreau">Henry David Thoreau</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Green_anarchism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Henry David Thoreau">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Henry_David_Thoreau" title="Henry David Thoreau">Henry David Thoreau</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Benjamin_D._Maxham_-_Henry_David_Thoreau_-_Restored_-_greyscale_-_straightened.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3027" data-file-width="2089" height="246" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Benjamin_D._Maxham_-_Henry_David_Thoreau_-_Restored_-_greyscale_-_straightened.jpg/170px-Benjamin_D._Maxham_-_Henry_David_Thoreau_-_Restored_-_greyscale_-_straightened.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Benjamin_D._Maxham_-_Henry_David_Thoreau_-_Restored_-_greyscale_-_straightened.jpg/255px-Benjamin_D._Maxham_-_Henry_David_Thoreau_-_Restored_-_greyscale_-_straightened.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Benjamin_D._Maxham_-_Henry_David_Thoreau_-_Restored_-_greyscale_-_straightened.jpg/340px-Benjamin_D._Maxham_-_Henry_David_Thoreau_-_Restored_-_greyscale_-_straightened.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Benjamin_D._Maxham_-_Henry_David_Thoreau_-_Restored_-_greyscale_-_straightened.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Henry_David_Thoreau" title="Henry David Thoreau">Henry David Thoreau</a>, influential early green anarchist who wrote <i><a href="/wiki/Walden" title="Walden">Walden</a></i></div></div></div>
<p>Anarchism started to have an ecological view mainly in the writings of American <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anarchist" title="Anarchist">anarchist</a> and <a href="/wiki/Transcendentalism" title="Transcendentalism">transcendentalist</a> <a href="/wiki/Henry_David_Thoreau" title="Henry David Thoreau">Henry David Thoreau</a>. In his book <i><a href="/wiki/Walden" title="Walden">Walden</a></i> he advocates <a href="/wiki/Simple_living" title="Simple living">simple living</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Self-sufficiency" title="Self-sufficiency">self-sufficiency</a> among natural surroundings in resistance to the advancement of <a href="/wiki/Industrial_civilization" title="Industrial civilization">industrial civilization</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thoreau_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thoreau-9">[9]</a></sup> The work is part personal declaration of independence, <a href="/wiki/Social_experiment" title="Social experiment">social experiment</a>, voyage of spiritual discovery, satire, and manual for self-reliance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> First published in 1854, it details Thoreau's experiences over the course of two years, two months, and two days in a cabin he built near <a href="/wiki/Walden_Pond" title="Walden Pond">Walden Pond</a>, amidst woodland owned by his friend and mentor <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Waldo_Emerson" title="Ralph Waldo Emerson">Ralph Waldo Emerson</a>, near <a href="/wiki/Concord,_Massachusetts" title="Concord, Massachusetts">Concord</a>, Massachusetts. The book compresses the time into a single calendar year and uses passages of four seasons to symbolize human development. By immersing himself in nature, Thoreau hoped to gain a more objective understanding of society through personal introspection. <a href="/wiki/Simple_living" title="Simple living">Simple living</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Self-sufficiency" title="Self-sufficiency">self-sufficiency</a> were Thoreau's other goals, and the whole project was inspired by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transcendentalist" title="Transcendentalist">transcendentalist</a> philosophy, a central theme of the American Romantic Period. As Thoreau made clear in his book, his cabin was not in wilderness but at the edge of town, about two miles (3.2 km) from his family home.
</p><p>As such "Many have seen in Thoreau one of the precursors of ecologism and <a href="/wiki/Anarcho-primitivism" title="Anarcho-primitivism">anarcho-primitivism</a> represented today in <a href="/wiki/John_Zerzan" title="John Zerzan">John Zerzan</a>. For <a href="/wiki/George_Woodcock" title="George Woodcock">George Woodcock</a> this attitude can be also motivated by certain idea of resistance to progress and of rejection of the growing materialism which is the nature of American society in the mid 19th century."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thoreau_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-Thoreau-9">[9]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_Zerzan" title="John Zerzan">John Zerzan</a> himself included the text "Excursions" (1863) by Thoreau in his edited compilation of writings called <i>Against civilization: Readings and reflections</i> from 1999.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zerzan2005_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zerzan2005-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id=".C3.89lis.C3.A9e_Reclus"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Élisée_Reclus">Élisée Reclus</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Green_anarchism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Élisée Reclus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/%C3%89lis%C3%A9e_Reclus" title="Élisée Reclus">Élisée Reclus</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:EliseeReclusNadar.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="502" data-file-width="348" height="289" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/EliseeReclusNadar.jpg/200px-EliseeReclusNadar.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/EliseeReclusNadar.jpg/300px-EliseeReclusNadar.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/6a/EliseeReclusNadar.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:EliseeReclusNadar.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/%C3%89lis%C3%A9e_Reclus" title="Élisée Reclus">Élisée Reclus</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/French_anarchist" title="French anarchist">French anarchist</a> geographer and <a href="/wiki/Environmentalist" title="Environmentalist">environmentalist</a></div></div></div><p>
Élisée Reclus (15 March 1830 – 4 July 1905), also known as Jacques Élisée Reclus, was a renowned <a href="/wiki/French_people" title="French people">French</a> <a href="/wiki/Geographer" title="Geographer">geographer</a>, writer and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anarchist" title="Anarchist">anarchist</a>. He produced his 19-volume masterwork <i>La Nouvelle Géographie universelle, la terre et les hommes</i> ("Universal Geography"), over a period of nearly 20 years (1875–1894). In 1892, he was awarded the prestigious Gold Medal of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soci%C3%A9t%C3%A9_de_G%C3%A9ographie" title="Société de Géographie">Paris Geographical Society</a> for this work, despite his having been banished from France because of his political activism. According to <a href="/wiki/Kirkpatrick_Sale" title="Kirkpatrick Sale">Kirkpatrick Sale</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sale_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sale-12">[12]</a></sup> </p><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>His geographical work, thoroughly researched and unflinchingly scientific, laid out a picture of human-nature interaction that we today would call <a href="/wiki/Bioregionalism" title="Bioregionalism">bioregionalism</a>. It showed, with more detail than anyone but a dedicated geographer could possibly absorb, how the ecology of a place determined the kinds of lives and livelihoods its denizens would have and thus how people could properly live in self-regarding and self-determined bioregions without the interference of large and centralized governments that always try to homogenize diverse geographical areas.
</p></blockquote>
<p>For the authors of <a href="/wiki/An_Anarchist_FAQ" title="An Anarchist FAQ">An Anarchist FAQ</a> Reclus "argued that a "secret harmony exists between the earth and the people whom it nourishes, and when imprudent societies let themselves violate this harmony, they always end up regretting it." Similarly, no contemporary ecologist would disagree with his comments that the "truly civilised man [and women] understands that his [or her] nature is bound up with the interest of all and with that of nature. He [or she] repairs the damage caused by his predecessors and works to improve his domain."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>Reclus advocated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nature_conservation" title="Nature conservation">nature conservation</a> and opposed meat-eating and cruelty to animals. He was a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vegetarian" title="Vegetarian">vegetarian</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vegetarian_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-vegetarian-14">[14]</a></sup> As a result, his ideas are seen by some historians as anticipating the modern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Social_ecology_(theory)" title="Social ecology (theory)">social ecology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Animal_rights" title="Animal rights">animal rights</a> movements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-demanding_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-demanding-15">[15]</a></sup> Shortly before his death, Reclus completed <i>L'Homme et la terre</i> (1905).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> In it, he added to his previous greater works by considering humanity's development relative to its geographical environment. Reclus was also an early proponent of <a href="/wiki/Naturism" title="Naturism">naturism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reclus1_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Reclus1-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Anarcho-naturism">Anarcho-naturism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Green_anarchism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Anarcho-naturism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Anarcho-naturism" title="Anarcho-naturism">Anarcho-naturism</a></div>
<p>In the late 19th century Anarchist naturism appeared as the union of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anarchist" title="Anarchist">anarchist</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Naturist" title="Naturist">naturist</a> philosophies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-naturismolibertario_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-naturismolibertario-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-naturismo.org_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-naturismo.org-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Mainly it had importance within <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Individualist_anarchist" title="Individualist anarchist">individualist anarchist</a> circles<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spanishind_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-spanishind-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aujourdhui_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-aujourdhui-20">[20]</a></sup> in Spain,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-naturismolibertario_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-naturismolibertario-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spanishind_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-spanishind-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-naturismo.org_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-naturismo.org-18">[18]</a></sup> France,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spanishind_6-3"><a href="#cite_note-spanishind-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-france_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-france-21">[21]</a></sup> Portugal,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-portugal_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-portugal-22">[22]</a></sup> and Cuba.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p>

<div class="NavFrame collapsed" style="border:none;padding:0"><div class="NavHead" style="font-size:105%;background:transparent;text-align:left;background:transparent;border-top:1px solid #aaa;text-align:center;"><a href="/wiki/History_of_anarchism" title="History of anarchism">History</a></div><div class="NavContent hlist" style="font-size:105%;padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;text-align:center">

<li><a href="/wiki/International_Conference_of_Rome_for_the_Social_Defense_Against_Anarchists" title="International Conference of Rome for the Social Defense Against Anarchists">International Conference of Rome</a></li>

</tr><tr><td class="plainlist" style="padding:0.3em 0.4em 0.3em;font-weight:bold;border-top: 1px solid #aaa; border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa;text-align:center;">
<ul><li><img alt="" data-file-height="456" data-file-width="456" height="15" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/BlackFlagSymbol.svg/15px-BlackFlagSymbol.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/BlackFlagSymbol.svg/23px-BlackFlagSymbol.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/BlackFlagSymbol.svg/30px-BlackFlagSymbol.svg.png 2x" width="15"/> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Anarchism" title="Portal:Anarchism">Anarchism portal</a></li>
<li><span style="font-weight:normal;"><a href="/wiki/Portal:Politics" title="Portal:Politics">Politics portal</a></span></li></ul></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Anarchism_sidebar" title="Template:Anarchism sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Anarchism_sidebar" title="Template talk:Anarchism sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Anarchism_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Anarcho-naturism advocated <a href="/wiki/Vegetarianism" title="Vegetarianism">vegetarianism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Free_love" title="Free love">free love</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nudism" title="Nudism">nudism</a> and an ecological world view within anarchist groups and outside them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-naturismolibertario_3-4"><a href="#cite_note-naturismolibertario-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aujourdhui_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-aujourdhui-20">[20]</a></sup>
Anarcho-naturism promoted an ecological worldview, small <a href="/wiki/Ecovillage" title="Ecovillage">ecovillages</a>, and most prominently nudism as a way to avoid the artificiality of the industrial mass society of modernity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-naturismo-iberica_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-naturismo-iberica-24">[24]</a></sup> Naturist individualist anarchists saw the individual in his biological, physical and psychological aspects and tried to eliminate social determinations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-naturismo-social_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-naturismo-social-25">[25]</a></sup>  Important promoters of this were <a href="/wiki/Henri_Zisly" title="Henri Zisly">Henri Zisly</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emile_Gravelle" title="Emile Gravelle">Emile Gravelle</a> who collaborated in <i>La Nouvelle Humanité</i> followed by <i>Le Naturien</i>, <i>Le Sauvage</i>, <i>L'Ordre Naturel</i>, &amp; <i>La Vie Naturelle</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-naturismo2_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-naturismo2-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="France">France</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Green_anarchism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: France">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4><p>
Richard D. Sonn comments on the influence of naturist views in the wider French anarchist movement: </p><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>In her memoir of her anarchist years that was serialized in <i>Le Matin</i> in 1913, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rirette_Maitrejean" title="Rirette Maitrejean">Rirette Maîtrejean</a> made much of the strange food regimens of some of the <i>compagnons</i>. [...] She described the "tragic bandits" of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bonnot_gang" title="Bonnot gang">Bonnot gang</a> as refusing to eat meat or drink wine, preferring plain water. Her humorous comments reflected the practices of the "naturist" wing of individualist anarchists who favored a simpler, more "natural" lifestyle centered on a vegetarian diet. In the 1920s, this wing was expressed by the journal <i>Le Néo-Naturien, Revue des Idées Philosophiques et Naturiennes</i>. Contributors condemned the fashion of smoking cigarettes, especially by young women; a long article of 1927 actually connected cigarette smoking with cancer! Others distinguished between vegetarians, who foreswore the eating of meat, from the stricter "vegetalians," who ate nothing but vegetables. An anarchist named G. Butaud, who made this distinction, opened a restaurant called the Foyer Végétalien in the nineteenth arrondissement in 1923. Other issues of the journal included vegetarian recipes. In 1925, when the young anarchist and future detective novelist Léo Malet arrived in Paris from Montpellier, he initially lodged with anarchists who operated another vegetarian restaurant that served only vegetables, with neither fish nor eggs. Nutritional concerns coincided with other means of encouraging health bodies, such as nudism and gymnastics. For a while in the 1920s, after they were released from jail for antiwar and birth-control activities, Jeanne and Eugène Humbert retreated to the relative safety of the "integral living" movement that promoted nude sunbathing and physical fitness, which were seen as integral aspects of health in the Greek sense of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gymnos" title="Gymnos">gymnos</a></i>, meaning nude. This back-to-nature, primitivist current was not a monopoly of the left; the same interests were echoed by right-wing Germans in the interwar era. In France, however, these proclivities were mostly associated with anarchists, insofar as they suggested an ideal of self-control and the rejection of social <a href="/wiki/Taboo" title="Taboo">taboos</a> and prejudices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Henri_Zisly">Henri Zisly</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Green_anarchism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Henri Zisly">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Henri_Zisly" title="Henri Zisly">Henri Zisly</a></div>
<p>Henri Zisly (born in Paris, November 2, 1872; died in 1945)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> was a <a href="/wiki/French_people" title="French people">French</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Individualist_anarchist" title="Individualist anarchist">individualist anarchist</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Naturist" title="Naturist">naturist</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[a]</a></sup> He participated alongside <a href="/wiki/Henri_Beylie" title="Henri Beylie">Henri Beylie</a> and  <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emile_Gravelle" title="Emile Gravelle">Émile Gravelle</a> in many journals such as <i>La Nouvelle Humanité</i> and <i>La Vie Naturelle</i>, which promoted <a href="/wiki/Anarcho-naturism" title="Anarcho-naturism">anarchist-naturism</a>. In 1902, he was one of the main initiators, alongside Georges Butaud and Sophie Zaïkowska, of the <a href="/wiki/Cooperative" title="Cooperative">cooperative</a> <i>Colonie de Vaux</i> established in <a href="/wiki/Ess%C3%B4mes-sur-Marne" title="Essômes-sur-Marne">Essômes-sur-Marne</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Aisne" title="Aisne">Aisne</a>.
</p><p>Zisly's political activity, "primarily aimed at supporting a return to 'natural life' through writing and practical involvement, stimulated lively confrontations within and outside the anarchist environment. Zisly vividly criticized progress and civilization, which he regarded as 'absurd, ignoble, and filthy.' He openly opposed industrialization, arguing that machines were inherently authoritarian, defended nudism, advocated a non-dogmatic and non-religious adherence to the 'laws of nature,' recommended a lifestyle based on limited needs and self-sufficiency, and disagreed with vegetarianism, which he considered 'anti-scientific.'"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boni_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-Boni-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cuba">Cuba</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Green_anarchism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Cuba">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The historian Kirwin R. Schaffer in his study of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cuban_anarchism" title="Cuban anarchism">Cuban anarchism</a> reports anarcho-naturism as "A third strand within the island's anarchist movement" alongside <a href="/wiki/Anarcho-communism" title="Anarcho-communism">anarcho-communism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anarcho-syndicalism" title="Anarcho-syndicalism">anarcho-syndicalism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-raforum.info_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-raforum.info-5">[5]</a></sup> Naturism was a global alternative health and lifestyle movement. Naturists focused on redefining one's life to live simply, eat cheap but nutritious vegetarian diets, and raise one's own food if possible. The countryside was posited as a romantic alternative to urban living, and some naturists even promoted what they saw as the healthful benefits of nudism. Globally, the naturist movement counted anarchists, liberals, and socialists as its followers. However, in Cuba a particular "anarchist" dimension evolved led by people like Adrián del Valle, who spearheaded the Cuban effort to shift naturism's focus away from only individual health to naturism having a "social emancipatory" function."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-raforum.info_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-raforum.info-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>Schaffer reports the influence that anarcho-naturism had outside naturists circles. So "For instance, nothing inherently prevented an anarcho-syndicalist in the Havana restaurant workers' union from supporting the alternative health care programs of the anarcho-naturists and seeing those alternative practices as "revolutionary."".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-raforum.info_5-3"><a href="#cite_note-raforum.info-5">[5]</a></sup> "Anarcho-naturists promoted a rural ideal, simple living, and being in harmony with Nature as ways to save the laborers from the increasingly industrialized character of Cuba. Besides promoting an early twentieth-century "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Back-to-the-land" title="Back-to-the-land">back-to-the-land</a>" movement, they used these romantic images of Nature to illustrate how far removed a capitalist industrialized Cuba had departed from an anarchist view of natural harmony."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-raforum.info_5-4"><a href="#cite_note-raforum.info-5">[5]</a></sup> The main propagandizer in Cuba of anarcho-naturism was the <a href="/wiki/Catalonia" title="Catalonia">Catalonia</a> born "Adrián del Valle (aka Palmiro de Lidia)...Over the following decades, Del Valle became a constant presence in not only the anarchist press that proliferated in Cuba but also mainstream literary publications...From 1912 to 1913 he edited the <a href="/wiki/Freethought" title="Freethought">freethinking</a> journal <i>El Audaz</i>. Then he began his largest publishing job by helping to found and edit the monthly alternative health magazine that followed the anarcho-naturist line <i>Pro-Vida</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-raforum.info_5-5"><a href="#cite_note-raforum.info-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Spain">Spain</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Green_anarchism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Spain">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Isaac_Puente.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="655" data-file-width="460" height="285" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Isaac_Puente.jpg/200px-Isaac_Puente.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Isaac_Puente.jpg/300px-Isaac_Puente.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Isaac_Puente.jpg/400px-Isaac_Puente.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Isaac_Puente.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Isaac_Puente" title="Isaac Puente">Isaac Puente</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anarchism_in_Spain" title="Anarchism in Spain">Spanish</a> <a href="/wiki/Anarcho-naturism" title="Anarcho-naturism">anarchist naturist</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anarcho-communist" title="Anarcho-communist">anarcho-communist</a></div></div></div><p>
Anarcho-naturism was quite important at the end of the 1920s in the <a href="/wiki/Anarchism_in_Spain" title="Anarchism in Spain">spanish anarchist movement</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-naturismo.org_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-naturismo.org-18">[18]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Anarchism_in_France" title="Anarchism in France">France</a>, later important propagandists of anarcho-naturism include <a href="/wiki/Henri_Zisly" title="Henri Zisly">Henri Zisly</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boni_29-2"><a href="#cite_note-Boni-29">[29]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emile_Gravelle" title="Emile Gravelle">Émile Gravelle</a> whose ideas were important in individualist anarchist circles in Spain, where <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Federico_Urales" title="Federico Urales">Federico Urales</a> (pseudonym of Joan Montseny) promoted the ideas of Gravelle and Zisly in <i><a href="/wiki/La_Revista_Blanca" title="La Revista Blanca">La Revista Blanca</a></i> (1898–1905):<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-naturismo1_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-naturismo1-31">[30]</a></sup> </p><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The linking role played by the Sol y Vida group was very important. The goal of this group was to take trips and enjoy the open air. The Naturist athenaeum, <i>Ecléctico</i>, in Barcelona, was the base from which the activities of the group were launched. First <i>Etica</i> and then <i><a href="/wiki/Iniciales" title="Iniciales">Iniciales</a></i>, which began in 1929, were the publications of the group, which lasted until the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Civil_War" title="Spanish Civil War">Spanish Civil War</a>. We must be aware that the naturist ideas expressed in them matched the desires that the libertarian youth had of breaking up with the conventions of the bourgeoisie of the time. That is what a young worker explained in a letter to <i>Iniciales</i>. He writes it under the odd pseudonym of <i>silvestre del campo</i> (wild man in the country). "I find great pleasure in being naked in the woods, bathed in light and air, two natural elements we cannot do without. By shunning the humble garment of an exploited person, (garments which, in my opinion, are the result of all the laws devised to make our lives bitter), we feel there no others left but just the natural laws. Clothes mean slavery for some and tyranny for others. Only the naked man who rebels against all norms, stands for anarchism, devoid of the prejudices of outfit imposed by our money-oriented society."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-naturismo.org_18-3"><a href="#cite_note-naturismo.org-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p></blockquote><p>
The "relation between Anarchism and Naturism gives way to the Naturist Federation, in July 1928, and to the lV Spanish Naturist Congress, in September 1929, both supported by the Libertarian Movement. However, in the short term, the Naturist and Libertarian movements grew apart in their conceptions of everyday life. The Naturist movement felt closer to the Libertarian individualism of some French theoreticians such as Henri Ner (real name of <a href="/wiki/Han_Ryner" title="Han Ryner">Han Ryner</a>) than to the revolutionary goals proposed by some Anarchist organisations such as the FAI, (<a href="/wiki/Federaci%C3%B3n_Anarquista_Ib%C3%A9rica" title="Federación Anarquista Ibérica">Federación Anarquista Ibérica</a>)".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-naturismo.org_18-4"><a href="#cite_note-naturismo.org-18">[18]</a></sup> This ecological tendency in Spanish anarchism was strong enough as to call the attention of the <a href="/wiki/Confederaci%C3%B3n_Nacional_del_Trabajo" title="Confederación Nacional del Trabajo">CNT</a>–<a href="/wiki/Federaci%C3%B3n_Anarquista_Ib%C3%A9rica" title="Federación Anarquista Ibérica">FAI</a> in Spain. <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Gu%C3%A9rin" title="Daniel Guérin">Daniel Guérin</a> in <i>Anarchism: From Theory to Practice</i> reports: </p><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Spanish <a href="/wiki/Anarcho-syndicalism" title="Anarcho-syndicalism">anarcho-syndicalism</a> had long been concerned to safeguard the autonomy of what it called "<a href="/wiki/Affinity_group" title="Affinity group">affinity groups</a>." There were many adepts of naturism and <a href="/wiki/Vegetarianism" title="Vegetarianism">vegetarianism</a> among its members, especially among the poor <a href="/wiki/Peasant" title="Peasant">peasants</a> of the south. Both these ways of living were considered suitable for the transformation of the human being in preparation for a libertarian society. At the Saragossa congress the members did not forget to consider the fate of groups of naturists and nudists, "unsuited to industrialization." As these groups would be unable to supply all their own needs, the congress anticipated that their delegates to the meetings of the confederation of communes would be able to negotiate special economic agreements with the other agricultural and industrial communes. On the eve of a vast, bloody, social transformation, the CNT did not think it foolish to try to meet the infinitely varied aspirations of individual human beings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[31]</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Isaac_Puente">Isaac Puente</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Green_anarchism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Isaac Puente">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Puente" title="Isaac Puente">Isaac Puente</a></div>
<p>Isaac Puente was an influential Spanish anarchist during the 1920s and 1930s and an important propagandist of anarcho-naturism,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cgt.es_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-cgt.es-33">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[33]</a></sup> was a militant of both the <a href="/wiki/Confederaci%C3%B3n_Nacional_del_Trabajo" title="Confederación Nacional del Trabajo">CNT</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anarcho-syndicalist" title="Anarcho-syndicalist">anarcho-syndicalist</a> trade union and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iberian_Anarchist_Federation" title="Iberian Anarchist Federation">Iberian Anarchist Federation</a>. He published the book <i>El Comunismo Libertario y otras proclamas insurreccionales y naturistas</i> (en:<i>Libertarian Communism and other insurrectionary and naturist proclamations</i>) in 1933, which sold around 100,000 copies,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[34]</a></sup> and wrote the final document for the Extraordinary Confederal Congress of Zaragoza of 1936 which established the main political line for the CNT for that year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[35]</a></sup> Puente was a doctor who approached his medical practice from a naturist point of view.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cgt.es_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-cgt.es-33">[32]</a></sup> He saw naturism as an integral solution for the working classes, alongside <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neo-Malthusianism" title="Neo-Malthusianism">Neo-Malthusianism</a>, and believed it concerned the living being while anarchism addressed the social being.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[36]</a></sup> He believed capitalist societies endangered the well-being of humans from both a socioeconomic and sanitary viewpoint, and promoted <a href="/wiki/Anarcho-communism" title="Anarcho-communism">anarcho-communism</a> alongside naturism as a solution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cgt.es_33-2"><a href="#cite_note-cgt.es-33">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_countries">Other countries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Green_anarchism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Other countries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Naturism also met <a href="/wiki/Anarchism_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Anarchism in the United Kingdom">anarchism in the United Kingdom</a>. "In many of the alternative communities established in Britain in the early 1900s nudism, anarchism, vegetarianism and free love were accepted as part of a politically radical way of life. In the 1920s the inhabitants of the anarchist community at Whiteway, near Stroud in Gloucestershire, shocked the conservative residents of the area with their shameless nudity."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[37]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Anarchism_in_Italy" title="Anarchism in Italy">Italy</a>, during the IX Congress of the <a href="/wiki/Italian_Anarchist_Federation" title="Italian Anarchist Federation">Italian Anarchist Federation</a> in <a href="/wiki/Carrara" title="Carrara">Carrara</a> in 1965, a group decided to split off from this organization and created the <i>Gruppi di Iniziativa Anarchica</i>. In the seventies, it was mostly composed of "veteran individualist anarchists with an orientation of <a href="/wiki/Anarcho-pacifism" title="Anarcho-pacifism">pacifism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anarcho-naturism" title="Anarcho-naturism">naturism</a>, etc,...".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[38]</a></sup> American anarcho-syndicalist <a href="/wiki/Sam_Dolgoff" title="Sam Dolgoff">Sam Dolgoff</a> shows some of the criticism that some people on the other anarchist currents at the time had for anarcho-naturist tendencies. "Speaking of life at the Stelton Colony of New York in the 1930s, noted with disdain that it, "like other colonies, was infested by vegetarians, naturists, nudists, and other cultists, who sidetracked true anarchist goals." One resident "always went barefoot, ate raw food, mostly nuts and raisins, and refused to use a tractor, being opposed to machinery, and he didn't want to abuse horses, so he dug the earth himself." Such self-proclaimed anarchists were in reality "ox-cart anarchists," Dolgoff said, "who opposed organization and wanted to return to a simpler life." In an interview with <a href="/wiki/Paul_Avrich" title="Paul Avrich">Paul Avrich</a> before his death, Dolgoff also grumbled, "I am sick and tired of these half-assed artists and poets who object to organization and want only to play with their belly buttons."".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Leo_Tolstoy_and_Tolstoyanism">Leo Tolstoy and Tolstoyanism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Green_anarchism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Leo Tolstoy and Tolstoyanism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Leo_Tolstoy" title="Leo Tolstoy">Leo Tolstoy</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tolstoyanism" title="Tolstoyanism">Tolstoyanism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:102px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ilya_Repin_-_Leo_Tolstoy_Barefoot_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="6490" data-file-width="2281" height="285" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Ilya_Repin_-_Leo_Tolstoy_Barefoot_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/100px-Ilya_Repin_-_Leo_Tolstoy_Barefoot_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Ilya_Repin_-_Leo_Tolstoy_Barefoot_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/150px-Ilya_Repin_-_Leo_Tolstoy_Barefoot_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Ilya_Repin_-_Leo_Tolstoy_Barefoot_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/200px-Ilya_Repin_-_Leo_Tolstoy_Barefoot_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" width="100"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ilya_Repin_-_Leo_Tolstoy_Barefoot_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Leo_Tolstoy" title="Leo Tolstoy">Leo Tolstoy</a> dressed in peasant clothing by <a href="/wiki/Ilya_Repin" title="Ilya Repin">Ilya Repin</a> (1901)</div></div></div><p>
Russian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christian_anarchist" title="Christian anarchist">Christian anarchist</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anarcho-pacifist" title="Anarcho-pacifist">anarcho-pacifist</a> Leo Tolstoy is also recognized as an early influence in green anarchism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-naturismolibertario_3-5"><a href="#cite_note-naturismolibertario-3">[3]</a></sup> The novelist was struck by the description of Christian, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddhist" title="Buddhist">Buddhist</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hindu" title="Hindu">Hindu</a> ascetic renunciation as being the path to holiness. After reading passages such as the following, which abound in Schopenhauer's ethical chapters, the Russian nobleman chose poverty and formal denial of the will: </p><blockquote><p>But this very necessity of involuntary suffering (by poor people) for eternal salvation is also expressed by that utterance of the Savior (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Matthew_19:24" title="Matthew 19:24">Matthew 19:24</a>): "It is easier for a camel to go through the eye of a needle, than for a rich man to enter into the kingdom of God." Therefore those who were greatly in earnest about their eternal salvation, chose <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Voluntary_poverty" title="Voluntary poverty">voluntary poverty</a> when fate had denied this to them and they had been born in wealth. Thus <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddha" title="Buddha">Buddha</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sakyamuni" title="Sakyamuni">Sakyamuni</a> was born a prince, but voluntarily took to the mendicant's staff; and <a href="/wiki/Francis_of_Assisi" title="Francis of Assisi">Francis of Assisi</a>, the founder of the <a href="/wiki/Mendicant_orders" title="Mendicant orders">mendicant orders</a> who, as a youngster at a ball, where the daughters of all the notabilities were sitting together, was asked: "Now Francis, will you not soon make your choice from these beauties?" and who replied: "I have made a far more beautiful choice!" "Whom?" "<i>La povertà</i> (poverty)": whereupon he abandoned every thing shortly afterwards and wandered through the land as a mendicant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[40]</a></sup> </p></blockquote>
<p>Despite his misgivings about <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anarchist_violence" title="Anarchist violence">anarchist violence</a>, Tolstoy took risks to circulate the prohibited publications of <a href="/wiki/Anarchism_in_Russia" title="Anarchism in Russia">anarchist thinkers in Russia</a>, and corrected the proofs of Kropotkin's "Words of a Rebel", illegally published in St Petersburg in 1906.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[41]</a></sup> Tolstoy was enthused by the economic thinking of <a href="/wiki/Henry_George" title="Henry George">Henry George</a>, incorporating it approvingly into later works such as <i><a href="/wiki/Resurrection_(novel)" title="Resurrection (novel)">Resurrection</a></i>, the book that played a major factor in his excommunication.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[42]</a></sup> Tolstoyans identify themselves as <a href="/wiki/Christian" title="Christian">Christians</a>, but do not generally belong to an institutional <a href="/wiki/Christian_Church" title="Christian Church">Church</a>. They attempt to live an <a href="/wiki/Asceticism" title="Asceticism">ascetic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Simple_living" title="Simple living">simple life</a>, preferring to be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vegetarian" title="Vegetarian">vegetarian</a>, non-smoking, <a href="/wiki/Teetotalism" title="Teetotalism">teetotal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chastity" title="Chastity">chaste</a>. Tolstoyans are considered <a href="/wiki/Christian_pacifism" title="Christian pacifism">Christian pacifists</a> and advocate <a href="/wiki/Nonresistance" title="Nonresistance">nonresistance</a> in all circumstances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[43]</a></sup> They do not support or participate in the <a href="/wiki/Government" title="Government">government</a> which they consider immoral, violent and corrupt. Tolstoy rejected the state (as it only exists on the basis of physical force) and all institutions that are derived from it - the police, law courts and army.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[44]</a></sup> Tolstoy influenced <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mohandas_Karamchand_Gandhi" title="Mohandas Karamchand Gandhi">Mohandas Karamchand Gandhi</a> who set up a cooperative colony called Tolstoy Farm near <a href="/wiki/Johannesburg" title="Johannesburg">Johannesburg</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>, having been inspired by Tolstoy's ideas. The colony comprising 1,100 acres (450 ha) was funded by the Gandhian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Herman_Kallenbach" title="Herman Kallenbach">Herman Kallenbach</a> and placed at the disposal of the <a href="/wiki/Satyagraha" title="Satyagraha">satyagrahis</a> from 1910.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[45]</a></sup> He also inspired similar communal experiments in the United States<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[46]</a></sup> where the residents were also influenced by the views of Henry George and <a href="/wiki/Edward_Bellamy" title="Edward Bellamy">Edward Bellamy</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[47]</a></sup> as well as in Russia,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[b]</a></sup> England<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[c]</a></sup> and the Netherlands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[d]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mid-20th_century">Mid-20th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Green_anarchism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Mid-20th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<p>Several anarchists from the mid-20th century like <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Read" title="Herbert Read">Herbert Read</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethel_Mannin" title="Ethel Mannin">Ethel Mannin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leopold_Kohr" title="Leopold Kohr">Leopold Kohr</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sale-foreword_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sale-foreword-55">[51]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paul_Goodman_(writer)" title="Paul Goodman (writer)">Paul Goodman</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rg_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-rg-56">[52]</a></sup> held proto-environmental views linked to their anarchism. Mannin's 1944 book <i>Bread and Roses: A Utopian Survey and Blue-Print</i> has been described by anarchist historian <a href="/wiki/Robert_Graham_(historian)" title="Robert Graham (historian)">Robert Graham</a> as setting forth "an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ecological" title="Ecological">ecological</a> vision in opposition to the prevailing and destructive <a href="/wiki/Industrial_society" title="Industrial society">industrial</a> organization of society".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rg_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-rg-56">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Leopold_Kohr">Leopold Kohr</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Green_anarchism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Leopold Kohr">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Leopold_Kohr" title="Leopold Kohr">Leopold Kohr</a></div><p>
Leopold Kohr (5 October 1909 in <a href="/wiki/Oberndorf_bei_Salzburg" title="Oberndorf bei Salzburg">Oberndorf bei Salzburg</a>, Austria – 26 February 1994 in <a href="/wiki/Gloucester" title="Gloucester">Gloucester</a>, England) was an <a href="/wiki/Economist" title="Economist">economist</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philosopher" title="Philosopher">philosopher</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Political_scientist" title="Political scientist">political scientist</a> known both for his opposition to the "cult of bigness" in social organization and as one of those who initiated the<i> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Small_is_Beautiful" title="Small is Beautiful">small is beautiful</a></i> movement. For almost twenty years he was Professor of Economics and Public Administration at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Puerto_Rico" title="University of Puerto Rico">University of Puerto Rico</a>. He described himself as a "<a href="/wiki/Philosophical_anarchism" title="Philosophical anarchism">philosophical anarchist</a>." In 1937, Kohr became a freelance correspondent during the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Civil_War" title="Spanish Civil War">Spanish Civil War</a>, where he was impressed by the limited, self-contained governments of the separatist states of <a href="/wiki/Catalonia" title="Catalonia">Catalonia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aragon" title="Aragon">Aragon</a>, as well as the small <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spanish_anarchist" title="Spanish anarchist">Spanish anarchist</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/City_states" title="City states">city states</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alcoy_(Spain)" title="Alcoy (Spain)">Alcoy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Caspe" title="Caspe">Caspe</a>. In his first published essay "Disunion Now: A Plea for a Society based upon Small Autonomous Units", published in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Commonweal_(UK)" title="Commonweal (UK)">Commonweal</a> in 1941, Kohr wrote about a Europe at war: "We have ridiculed the many little states, now we are terrorized by their few successors."  He called for the breakup of Europe into hundreds of city states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sale-foreword_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sale-foreword-55">[51]</a></sup>  Kohr developed his ideas in a series of books, including <i>The Breakdown of Nations</i> (1957), <i>Development without Aid</i> (1973) and <i>The Overdeveloped Nations</i> (1977).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rightlivelihood_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-rightlivelihood-57">[53]</a></sup> From Leopold Kohr's most popular work <i>The Breakdown of Nations</i>: </p><blockquote><p>[...] there seems to be only one cause behind all forms of social misery: bigness. Oversimplified as this may seem, we shall find the idea more easily acceptable if we consider that bigness, or oversize, is really much more than just a social problem. It appears to be the one and only problem permeating all creation. Whenever something is wrong, something is too big.  [...] And if the body of a people becomes diseased with the fever of aggression, brutality, collectivism, or massive idiocy, it is not because it has fallen victim to bad leadership or mental derangement. It is because human beings, so charming as individuals or in small aggregations, have been welded into overconcentrated social units.</p></blockquote>
<p>Later in his academic and writing career he protested the "cult of bigness" and economic growth and promoted the concept of human scale and small community life. He argued that massive external aid to poorer nations stifled local initiatives and participation. His vision called for a dissolution of centralized political and economic structures in favor of local control.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rightlivelihood_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-rightlivelihood-57">[53]</a></sup> Kohr was an important inspiration to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Green_movement" title="Green movement">Green</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bioregionalism" title="Bioregionalism">bioregional</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fourth_World" title="Fourth World">Fourth World</a>, <a href="/wiki/Decentralization" title="Decentralization">decentralist</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Anarchism" title="Anarchism">anarchist</a> movements, Kohr contributed often to <a href="/wiki/John_Papworth" title="John Papworth">John Papworth</a>'s `Journal for the Fourth World', <a href="/wiki/Resurgence_%26_Ecologist" title="Resurgence &amp; Ecologist">Resurgence</a>. One of Kohr's students was economist <a href="/wiki/E._F._Schumacher" title="E. F. Schumacher">E. F. Schumacher</a>, another prominent influence on these movements, whose <a href="/wiki/Bestseller" title="Bestseller">best selling</a> book <i><a href="/wiki/Small_Is_Beautiful" title="Small Is Beautiful">Small Is Beautiful</a></i> took its title from one of Kohr's core principles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-Obit_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-Obit-58">[54]</a></sup>  Similarly, his ideas inspired <a href="/wiki/Kirkpatrick_Sale" title="Kirkpatrick Sale">Kirkpatrick Sale</a>'s books <i>Human Scale</i> (1980) and <i>Dwellers in the Land: The Bioregional Vision</i> (1985). Sale arranged the first American publication of <i>The Breakdown of Nations</i> in 1978 and wrote the foreword.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sale-foreword_55-2"><a href="#cite_note-Sale-foreword-55">[51]</a></sup>
</p>
